one of the principal characteristics which make the real numbers superior
to the rational numbers for the purposes of doing analysis (taking limits,
taking derivatives and integrals, ï¬nding zeroes of functions, that kind of
thing), as we shall see in later chapters.

6.6
Subsequences
This chapter has been devoted to the study of sequences (an)âˆ
n=1 of real
numbers, and their limits. Some sequences were convergent to a single
limit, while others had multiple limit points. For instance, the sequence
1.1, 0.1, 1.01, 0.01, 1.001, 0.001, 1.0001, . . .
has two limit points at 0 and 1 (which are incidentally also the lim inf
and lim sup respectively), but is not actually convergent (since the lim
sup and lim inf are not equal).
However, while this sequence is not
convergent, it does appear to contain convergent components; it seems
to be a mixture of two convergent subsequences, namely
1.1, 1.01, 1.001, . . .
and
0.1, 0.01, 0.001, . . . .
To make this notion more precise, we need a notion of subsequence.
Deï¬nition 6.6.1 (Subsequences). Let (an)âˆ
n=0 and (bn)âˆ
n=0 be sequences
of real numbers. We say that (bn)âˆ
n=0 is a subsequence of (an)âˆ
n=0 iï¬€
there exists a function f : N â†’N which is strictly increasing (i.e.,
f(n + 1) > f(n) for all n âˆˆN) such that
bn = af(n) for all n âˆˆN.

150
6.
Limits of sequences
Example 6.6.2. If (an)âˆ
n=0 is a sequence, then (a2n)âˆ
n=0 is a subse-
quence of (an)âˆ
n=0, since the function f : N â†’N deï¬ned by f(n) := 2n
is a strictly increasing function from N to N. Note that we do not as-
sume f to be bijective, although it is necessarily injective (why?). More
informally, the sequence
a0, a2, a4, a6, . . .
is a subsequence of
a0, a1, a2, a3, a4, . . . .
Example 6.6.3. The two sequences
1.1, 1.01, 1.001, . . .
and
0.1, 0.01, 0.001, . . .
mentioned earlier are both subsequences of
1.1, 0.1, 1.01, 0.01, 1.001, 1.0001, . . .
The property of being a subsequence is reï¬‚exive and transitive,
though not symmetric:
Lemma 6.6.4. Let (an)âˆ
n=0, (bn)âˆ
n=0, and (cn)âˆ
n=0 be sequences of real

(bn)âˆ

n=0 is a subsequence of (an)âˆ
n=0.
Proof. See Exercise 6.6.1.
We now relate the concept of subsequences to the concept of limits
and limit points.
Proposition 6.6.5 (Subsequences related to limits). Let (an)âˆ
n=0 be a
sequence of real numbers, and let L be a real number. Then the following
two statements are logically equivalent (each one implies the other):
(a) The sequence (an)âˆ
n=0 converges to L.
(b) Every subsequence of (an)âˆ
n=0 converges to L.
Proof. See Exercise 6.6.4.

6.6.
Subsequences
151
Proposition 6.6.6 (Subsequences related to limit points). Let (an)âˆ
n=0
be a sequence of real numbers, and let L be a real number. Then the
following two statements are logically equivalent.
(a) L is a limit point of (an)âˆ
n=0.
(b) There exists a subsequence of (an)âˆ
n=0 which converges to L.
Proof. See Exercise 6.6.5.
Remark 6.6.7. The above two propositions give a sharp contrast be-
tween the notion of a limit, and that of a limit point. When a sequence
has a limit L, then all subsequences also converge to L. But when a
sequence has L as a limit point, then only some subsequences converge
to L.
We can now prove an important theorem in real analysis, due to
Bernard Bolzano (1781â€“1848) and Karl Weierstrass (1815â€“1897): every
bounded sequence has a convergent subsequence.
Theorem 6.6.8 (Bolzano-Weierstrass theorem). Let (an)âˆ
n=0 be a
bounded sequence (i.e., there exists a real number M > 0 such that
|an| â‰¤M for all n âˆˆN). Then there is at least one subsequence of
(an)âˆ
n=0 which converges.
Proof. Let L be the limit superior of the sequence (an)âˆ
n=0. Since we have
âˆ’M â‰¤an â‰¤M for all natural numbers n, it follows from the comparison
principle (Lemma 6.4.13) that âˆ’M â‰¤L â‰¤M. In particular, L is a real
number (not +âˆor âˆ’âˆ). By Proposition 6.4.12(e), L is thus a limit
point of (an)âˆ
n=0. Thus by Proposition 6.6.6, there exists a subsequence
of (an)âˆ
n=0 which converges (in fact, it converges to L).
Note that we could as well have used the limit inferior instead of the
limit superior in the above argument.
Remark 6.6.9. The Bolzano-Weierstrass theorem says that if a se-
quence is bounded, then eventually it has no choice but to converge
in some places; it has â€œno roomâ€ to spread out and stop itself from
acquiring limit points. It is not true for unbounded sequences; for in-
stance, the sequence 1, 2, 3, . . . has no convergent subsequences whatso-
ever (why?). In the language of topology, this means that the interval
{x âˆˆR : âˆ’M â‰¤x â‰¤M} is compact, whereas an unbounded set such as
the real line R is not compact. The distinction between compact sets

152

and non-compact sets will be very important in later chapters - of similar
importance to the distinction between ï¬nite sets and inï¬nite sets.

Remark 7.1.2. The diï¬€erence between â€œsumâ€ and â€œseriesâ€ is a subtle
linguistic one. Strictly speaking, a series is an expression of the form
n
i=m ai; this series is mathematically (but not semantically) equal to a
real number, which is then the sum of that series. For instance, 1 + 2 +
3 + 4 + 5 is a series, whose sum is 15; if one were to be very picky about
semantics, one would not consider 15 a series and one would not consider
1 + 2 + 3 + 4 + 5 a sum, despite the two expressions having the same
value. However, we will not be very careful about this distinction as it is
purely linguistic and has no bearing on the mathematics; the expressions
1 + 2 + 3 + 4 + 5 and 15 are the same number, and thus mathematically
interchangeable, in the sense of the axiom of substitution (see Section
A.7), even if they are not semantically interchangeable.
Remark 7.1.3. Note that the variable i (sometimes called the index of
summation) is a bound variable (sometimes called a dummy variable);

7.2
Inï¬nite series
We are now ready to sum inï¬nite series.
Deï¬nition 7.2.1 (Formal inï¬nite series). A (formal) inï¬nite series is
any expression of the form

where m is an integer, and an is a real number for any integer n â‰¥m.
We sometimes write this series as

At present, this series is only deï¬ned formally; we have not set this
sum equal to any real number; the notation am +am+1 +am+2 +. . . is of
course designed to look very suggestively like a sum, but is not actually
a ï¬nite sum because of the â€œ. . .â€ symbol. To rigorously deï¬ne what the
series actually sums to, we need another deï¬nition.

Deï¬nition 7.2.2 (Convergence of series). Let âˆ

Surprisingly, when the series is not absolutely convergent, then the
rearrangements are very badly behaved.
Example 7.4.4. Consider the series
1/3 âˆ’1/4 + 1/5 âˆ’1/6 + 1/7 âˆ’1/8 + . . . .

7.4.
Rearrangement of series
177
This series is not absolutely convergent (why?), but is conditionally
convergent by the alternating series test, and in fact the sum can
be seen to converge to a positive number (in fact, it converges to
ln(2) âˆ’1/2 = 0.193147 . . ., see Example 11.25.7). Basically, the reason
why the sum is positive is because the quantities (1/3âˆ’1/4), (1/5âˆ’1/6),
(1/7 âˆ’1/8) are all positive, which can then be used to show that every
partial sum is positive. (Why? you have to break into two cases, de-
pending on whether there are an even or odd number of terms in the
partial sum.)
If, however, we rearrange the series to have two negative terms to
each positive term, thus
1/3 âˆ’1/4 âˆ’1/6 + 1/5 âˆ’1/8 âˆ’1/10 + 1/7 âˆ’1/12 âˆ’1/14 + . . .
then the partial sums quickly become negative (this is because (1/3 âˆ’
1/4 âˆ’1/6), (1/5 âˆ’1/8 âˆ’1/9), and more generally (1/(2n + 1) âˆ’1/4n âˆ’
1/(4n + 2)) are all negative), and so this series converges to a negative
quantity; in fact, it converges to
(ln(2) âˆ’1)/2 = âˆ’.153426 . . . .
There is in fact a surprising result of Riemann, which shows that a series
which is conditionally convergent but not absolutely convergent can in
fact be rearranged to converge to any value (or rearranged to diverge,
in fact - see Exercise 8.2.6); see Theorem 8.2.8.
To summarize, rearranging series is safe when the series is absolutely
convergent, but is somewhat dangerous otherwise. (This is not to say
that rearranging a series that is not absolutely convergent necessarily
gives you the wrong answer - for instance, in theoretical physics one often
performs similar maneuvres, and one still (usually) obtains a correct
answer at the end - but doing so is risky, unless it is backed by a rigorous
result such as Proposition 7.4.3.)

7.5
The root and ratio tests
Now we can state and prove the famous root and ratio tests for conver-
gence.
Theorem 7.5.1 (Root test). Let âˆ
n=m an be a series of real numbers,
and let Î± := lim supnâ†’âˆ|an|1/n.
(a) If Î± < 1, then the series âˆ
n=m an is absolutely convergent (and
hence conditionally convergent).
(b) If Î± > 1, then the series âˆ
n=m an is not conditionally convergent
(and hence cannot be absolutely convergent either).
(c) If Î± = 1, we cannot assert any conclusion.
Proof. First suppose that Î± < 1.
Note that we must have Î± â‰¥0,
since |an|1/n â‰¥0 for every n. Then we can ï¬nd an Îµ > 0 such that
0 < Î± + Îµ < 1 (for instance, we can set Îµ := (1 âˆ’Î±)/2). By Proposition
6.4.12(a), there exists an N â‰¥m such that |an|1/n â‰¤Î± + Îµ for all
n â‰¥N. In other words, we have |an| â‰¤(Î± + Îµ)n for all n â‰¥N. But
from the geometric series we have that âˆ
n=N(Î± + Îµ)n is absolutely
convergent, since 0 < Î± + Îµ < 1 (note that the fact that we start from
N is irrelevant by Proposition 7.2.14(c)). Thus by the comparison test,
we see that âˆ
n=N an is absolutely convergent, and thus âˆ
n=m an is
absolutely convergent, by Proposition 7.2.14(c) again.
Now suppose that Î± > 1. Then by Proposition 6.4.12(b), we see
that for every N â‰¥m there exists an n â‰¥N such that |an|1/n â‰¥1,
and hence that |an| â‰¥1. In particular, (an)âˆ
n=N is not 1-close to 0 for
any N, and hence (an)âˆ
n=m is not eventually 1-close to 0. In particular,
(an)âˆ
n=m does not converge to zero. Thus by the zero test, âˆ
n=m an is
not conditionally convergent.
For Î± = 1, see Exercise 7.5.3.
The root test is phrased using the limit superior, but of course if
limnâ†’âˆ|an|1/n converges then the limit is the same as the limit superior.
Thus one can phrase the root test using the limit instead of the limit
superior, but only when the limit exists.
The root test is sometimes diï¬ƒcult to use; however we can replace
roots by ratios using the following lemma.

Chapter 8
Inï¬nite sets
We now return to the study of set theory, and speciï¬cally to the study
of cardinality of sets which are inï¬nite (i.e., sets which do not have
cardinality n for any natural number n), a topic which was initiated in
Section 3.6.
8.1
Countability
From Proposition 3.6.14(c) we know that if X is a ï¬nite set, and Y is
a proper subset of X, then Y does not have equal cardinality with X.
However, this is not the case for inï¬nite sets. For instance, from Theorem
3.6.12 we know that the set N of natural numbers is inï¬nite. The set
Nâˆ’{0} is also inï¬nite, thanks to Proposition 3.6.14(a) (why?), and is a
proper subset of N. However, the set N âˆ’{0}, despite being â€œsmallerâ€
than N, still has the same cardinality as N, because the function f :
N â†’Nâˆ’{0} deï¬ned by f(n) := n+1, is a bijection from N to Nâˆ’{0}.
(Why?) This is one characteristic of inï¬nite sets; see Exercise 8.1.1.
We now distinguish two types of inï¬nite sets: the countable sets and
the uncountable sets.
Deï¬nition 8.1.1 (Countable sets). A set X is said to be countably
inï¬nite (or just countable) iï¬€it has equal cardinality with the natural
numbers N.
A set X is said to be at most countable iï¬€it is either
countable or ï¬nite. We say that a set is uncountable if it is inï¬nite but
not countable.
Remark 8.1.2. Countably inï¬nite sets are also called denumerable sets.
Examples 8.1.3. From the preceding discussion we see that N is count-
able, and so is N âˆ’{0}. Another example of a countable set is the even
181
 Springer Science+Business Media Singapore 2016 and Hindustan Book Agency 2015
T. Tao, Analysis I, Texts and Readings in Mathematics 37,
DOI 10.1007/978-981-10-1789-6_8

182
8.
Inï¬nite sets
natural numbers {2n : n âˆˆN}, since the function f(n) := 2n provides a
bijection between N and the even natural numbers (why?).
Let X be a countable set. Then, by deï¬nition, we know that there
exists a bijection f : N â†’X. Thus, every element of X can be written
in the form f(n) for exactly one natural number n. Informally, we thus
have
X = {f(0), f(1), f(2), f(3), . . .}.
Thus, a countable set can be arranged in a sequence, so that we have
a zeroth element f(0), followed by a ï¬rst element f(1), then a sec-
ond element f(2), and so forth, in such a way that all these elements
f(0), f(1), f(2), . . . are all distinct, and together they ï¬ll out all of X.
(This is why these sets are called countable; because we can literally
count them one by one, starting from f(0), then f(1), and so forth.)
Viewed in this way, it is clear why the natural numbers
N = {0, 1, 2, 3, . . .},
the positive integers
N âˆ’{0} = {1, 2, 3, . . .},
and the even natural numbers
{0, 2, 4, 6, 8, . . .}
are countable. However, it is not as obvious whether the integers
Z = {. . . , âˆ’3, âˆ’2, âˆ’1, 0, 1, 2, 3, . . .}
or the rationals
Q = {0, 1/4, âˆ’2/3, . . .}

Proposition 8.1.4 (Well ordering principle). Let X be a non-empty
subset of the natural numbers N. Then there exists exactly one element
n âˆˆX such that n â‰¤m for all m âˆˆX. In other words, every non-empty
set of natural numbers has a minimum element.
Proof. See Exercise 8.1.2.
We will refer to the element n given by the well-ordering principle
as the minimum of X, and write it as min(X). Thus for instance the
minimum of the set {2, 4, 6, 8, . . .} is 2. This minimum is clearly the
same as the inï¬mum of X, as deï¬ned in Deï¬nition 5.5.10 (why?).
Proposition 8.1.5. Let X be an inï¬nite subset of the natural numbers
N. Then there exists a unique bijection f : N â†’X which is increasing,
in the sense that f(n + 1) > f(n) for all n âˆˆN. In particular, X has
equal cardinality with N and is hence countable.
Proof. We will give an incomplete sketch of the proof, with some gaps
marked by a question mark (?); these gaps will be ï¬lled in Exercise 8.1.3.
We now deï¬ne a sequence a0, a1, a2, . . . of natural numbers recur-
sively by the formula
an := min{x âˆˆX : x Ì¸= am for all m < n}.
Intuitively speaking, a0 is the smallest element of X; a1 is the second
smallest element of X, i.e., the smallest element of X once a0 is removed;
a2 is the third smallest element of X; and so forth. Observe that in order
to deï¬ne an, one only needs to know the values of am for all m < n, so
this deï¬nition is recursive. Also, since X is inï¬nite, the set {x âˆˆX :
x Ì¸= am for all m < n} is inï¬nite(?), hence non-empty. Thus by the well-
ordering principle, the minimum, min{x âˆˆX : x Ì¸= am for all m < n} is
always well-deï¬ned.
One can show(?) that an is an increasing sequence, i.e.
a0 < a1 < a2 < . . .
and in particular that(?) an Ì¸= am for all n Ì¸= m. Also, we have(?)
an âˆˆX for each natural number n.
Now deï¬ne the function f : N â†’X by f(n) := an.
From the
previous paragraph we know that f is one-to-one. Now we show that f
is onto. In other words, we claim that for every x âˆˆX, there exists an
n such that an = x.

8.

Let x âˆˆX. Suppose for sake of contradiction that an Ì¸= x for every
natural number n. Then this implies(?) that x is an element of the
set {x âˆˆX : x Ì¸= am for all m < n} for all n.
By deï¬nition of an,
this implies that x â‰¥an for every natural number n. However, since
an is an increasing sequence, we have an â‰¥n (?), and hence x â‰¥n for
every natural number n. In particular we have x â‰¥x + 1, which is a
contradiction. Thus we must have an = x for some natural number n,
and hence f is onto.
Since f : N â†’X is both one-to-one and onto, it is a bijection.
We have thus found at least one increasing bijection f from N to X.
Now suppose for sake of contradiction that there was at least one other
increasing bijection g from N to X which was not equal to f. Then the
set {n âˆˆN : g(n) Ì¸= f(n)} is non-empty, and deï¬ne m := min{n âˆˆN :
g(n) Ì¸= f(n)}, thus in particular g(m) Ì¸= f(m) = am, and g(n) = f(n) =
an for all n < m. But we then must have(?)
g(m) = min{x âˆˆX : x Ì¸= at for all t < m} = am,
a contradiction. Thus there is no other increasing bijection from N to
X other than f.
Since ï¬nite sets are at most countable by deï¬nition, we thus have
Corollary 8.1.6. All subsets of the natural numbers are at most count-
able.
Corollary 8.1.7. If X is an at most countable set, and Y is a subset
of X, then Y is at most countable.
Proof. If X is ï¬nite then this follows from Proposition 3.6.14(c), so
assume X is countable. Then there is a bijection f : X â†’N between
X and N. Since Y is a subset of X, and f is a bijection from X and
N, then when we restrict f to Y , we obtain a bijection between Y and
f(Y ). (Why is this a bijection?) Thus f(Y ) has equal cardinality with
Y . But f(Y ) is a subset of N, and hence at most countable by Corollary
8.1.6. Hence Y is also at most countable.
Proposition 8.1.8. Let Y be a set, and let f : N â†’Y be a function.
Then f(N) is at most countable.
Proof. See Exercise 8.1.4.

To summarize, any subset or image of a countable set is at most
countable, and any ï¬nite union of countable sets is still countable. We
can now establish countability of the integers.
Corollary 8.1.11. The integers Z are countable.
Proof. We already know that the set N = {0, 1, 2, 3, . . .} of natural num-
bers are countable. The set âˆ’N deï¬ned by
âˆ’N := {âˆ’n : n âˆˆN} = {0, âˆ’1, âˆ’2, âˆ’3, . . .}
is also countable, since the map f(n) := âˆ’n is a bijection between N
and this set. Since the integers are the union of N and âˆ’N, the claim
follows from Proposition 8.1.10
To establish countability of the rationals, we need to relate count-
ability with Cartesian products. In particular, we need to show that the
set N Ã— N is countable. We ï¬rst need a preliminary lemma:
Lemma 8.1.12. The set
A := {(n, m) âˆˆN Ã— N : 0 â‰¤m â‰¤n}
is countable.
Proof. Deï¬ne the sequence a0, a1, a2, . . . recursively by setting a0 := 0,
and an+1 := an + n + 1 for all natural numbers n. Thus
a0 = 0; a1 = 0 + 1; a2 = 0 + 1 + 2; a3 = 0 + 1 + 2 + 3; . . . .
By induction one can show that an is increasing, i.e., that an > am
whenever n > m (why?).
Now deï¬ne the function f : A â†’N by
f(n, m) := an + m.

186
8.
Inï¬nite sets
We claim that f is one-to-one. In other words, if (n, m) and (nâ€², mâ€²) are
any two distinct elements of A, then we claim that f(n, m) Ì¸= f(nâ€², mâ€²).
To prove this claim, let (n, m) and (nâ€², mâ€²) be two distinct elements of
A. There are three cases: nâ€² = n, nâ€² > n, and nâ€² < n. First suppose that
nâ€² = n. Then we must have m Ì¸= mâ€², otherwise (n, m) and (nâ€², mâ€²) would
not be distinct. Thus an + m Ì¸= an + mâ€², and hence f(n, m) Ì¸= f(nâ€², mâ€²),
as desired.
Now suppose that nâ€² > n. Then nâ€² â‰¥n + 1, and hence
f(nâ€², mâ€²) = anâ€² + mâ€² â‰¥anâ€² â‰¥an+1 = an + n + 1.

f(nâ€², mâ€²) â‰¥an + n + 1 > an + m = f(n, m),
and thus f(nâ€², mâ€²) Ì¸= f(n, m).
The case nâ€² < n is proven similarly, by switching the rË†oles of n and
nâ€² in the previous argument. Thus we have shown that f is one-to-one.
Thus f is a bijection from A to f(A), and so A has equal cardinality
with f(A). But f(A) is a subset of N, and hence by Corollary 8.1.6
f(A) is at most countable. Therefore A is at most countable. But, A
is clearly not ï¬nite. (Why? Hint: if A was ï¬nite, then every subset of
A would be ï¬nite, and in particular {(n, 0) : n âˆˆN} would be ï¬nite,
but this is clearly countably inï¬nite, a contradiction.) Thus, A must be
countable.
Corollary 8.1.13. The set N Ã— N is countable.
Proof. We already know that the set
A := {(n, m) âˆˆN Ã— N : 0 â‰¤m â‰¤n}

Corollary 8.1.14. If X and Y are countable, then X Ã— Y is countable.
Proof. See Exercise 8.1.8.

8.3
Uncountable sets
We have just shown that a lot of inï¬nite sets are countable - even such
sets as the rationals, for which it is not obvious how to arrange as a
sequence. After such examples, one may begin to hope that other inï¬nite
sets, such as the real numbers, are also countable - after all, the real
numbers are nothing more than (formal) limits of the rationals, and
weâ€™ve already shown the rationals are countable, so it seems plausible
that the reals are also countable.
It was thus a great shock when Georg Cantor (1845â€“1918) showed
in 1873 that certain sets - including the real numbers R are in fact
uncountable - no matter how hard you try, you cannot arrange the real
numbers R as a sequence a0, a1, a2, . . .. (Of course, the real numbers R
can contain many inï¬nite sequences, e.g., the sequence 0, 1, 2, 3, 4, . . ..
However, what Cantor proved is that no such sequence can ever exhaust
the real numbers; no matter what sequence of real numbers you choose,
there will always be some real numbers that are not covered by that
sequence.)
Recall from Remark 3.4.10 that if X is a set, then the power set of
X, denoted 2X := {A : A âŠ†X}, is the set of all subsets of X. Thus for
instance 2{1,2} = {âˆ…, {1}, {2}, {1, 2}}. The reason for the notation 2X is
given in Exercise 8.3.1.
Theorem 8.3.1 (Cantorâ€™s theorem). Let X be an arbitrary set (ï¬nite
or inï¬nite). Then the sets X and 2X cannot have equal cardinality.
Proof. Suppose for sake of contradiction that the sets X and 2X had
equal cardinality. Then there exists a bijection f : X â†’2X between X
and the power set of X. Now consider the set
A := {x âˆˆX : x Ì¸âˆˆf(x)}.
Note that this set is well-deï¬ned since f(x) is an element of 2X and is

196
8.
Inï¬nite sets
hence a subset of X. Clearly A is a subset of X, hence is an element
of 2X. Since f is a bijection, there must therefore exist x âˆˆX such
that f(x) = A. There are now two cases, depending on whether x âˆˆA
or x Ì¸âˆˆA. If x âˆˆA, then by deï¬nition of A we have x Ì¸âˆˆf(x), hence
x Ì¸âˆˆA, a contradiction. But if x Ì¸âˆˆA, then x Ì¸âˆˆf(x), hence by deï¬nition
of A we have x âˆˆA, a contradiction. Thus in either case we have a
contradiction.
Remark 8.3.2. The reader should compare the proof of Cantorâ€™s the-
orem with the statement of Russellâ€™s paradox (Section 3.2). The point
is that a bijection between X and 2X would come dangerously close to
the concept of a set X â€œcontaining itselfâ€.
Corollary 8.3.3. 2N is uncountable.
Proof. By Theorem 8.3.1, 2N cannot have equal cardinality with N,
hence is either uncountable or ï¬nite. However, 2N contains as a subset
the set of singletons {{n} : n âˆˆN}, which is clearly bijective to N
and hence countably inï¬nite. Thus 2N cannot be ï¬nite (by Proposition
3.6.14), and is hence uncountable.
Cantorâ€™s theorem has the following important (and unintuitive) con-
sequence.

8.4
The axiom of choice
We now discuss the ï¬nal axiom of the standard Zermelo-Fraenkel-Choice
system of set theory, namely the axiom of choice. We have delayed intro-
ducing this axiom for a while now, to demonstrate that a large portion of
the foundations of analysis can be constructed without appealing to this
axiom. However, in many further developments of the theory, it is very
convenient (and in some cases even essential) to employ this powerful
axiom. On the other hand, the axiom of choice can lead to a number
of unintuitive consequences (for instance the Banach-Tarski paradox, a
simpliï¬ed version of which we will encounter in Section 11.43), and can

8.4.
The axiom of choice
199
lead to proofs that are philosophically somewhat unsatisfying. Never-
theless, the axiom is almost universally accepted by mathematicians.
One reason for this conï¬dence is a theorem due to the great logician
Kurt GÂ¨odel, who showed that a result proven using the axiom of choice
will never contradict a result proven without the axiom of choice (unless
all the other axioms of set theory are themselves inconsistent, which is
highly unlikely). More precisely, GÂ¨odel demonstrated that the axiom
of choice is undecidable; it can neither be proved nor disproved from
the other axioms of set theory, so long as those axioms are themselves
consistent. (From a set of inconsistent axioms one can prove that ev-
ery statement is both true and false.) In practice, this means that any
â€œreal-lifeâ€ application of analysis (more precisely, any application in-
volving only â€œdecidableâ€ questions) which can be rigorously supported
using the axiom of choice, can also be rigorously supported without the
axiom of choice, though in many cases it would take a much more com-
plicated and lengthier argument to do so if one were not allowed to use
the axiom of choice. Thus one can view the axiom of choice as a con-
venient and safe labour-saving device in analysis. In other disciplines
of mathematics, notably in set theory in which many of the questions
are not decidable, the issue of whether to accept the axiom of choice is
more open to debate, and involves some philosophical concerns as well
as mathematical and logical ones. However, we will not discuss these
issues in this text.
We begin by generalizing the notion of ï¬nite Cartesian products from
Deï¬nition 3.5.7 to inï¬nite Cartesian products.
Deï¬nition 8.4.1 (Inï¬nite Cartesian products). Let I be a set (possibly

Recall from Lemma 3.5.12 that if X1, . . . , Xn were any ï¬nite collec-

Axiom 8.1 (Choice). Let I be a set, and for each Î± âˆˆI, let XÎ± be
a non-empty set. Then 
Î±âˆˆI XÎ± is also non-empty. In other words,
there exists a function (xÎ±)Î±âˆˆI which assigns to each Î± âˆˆI an element
xÎ± âˆˆXÎ±.
Remark 8.4.3. The intuition behind this axiom is that given a (pos-
sibly inï¬nite) collection of non-empty sets XÎ±, one should be able to
choose a single element xÎ± from each one, and then form the possibly
inï¬nite tuple (xÎ±)Î±âˆˆI from all the choices one has made. On one hand,
this is a very intuitively appealing axiom; in some sense one is just apply-
ing Lemma 3.1.6 over and over again. On the other hand, the fact that
one is making an inï¬nite number of arbitrary choices, with no explicit
rule as to how to make these choices, is a little disconcerting. Indeed,
there are many theorems proven using the axiom of choice which assert
the abstract existence of some object x with certain properties, with-
out saying at all what that object is, or how to construct it. Thus the
axiom of choice can lead to proofs which are non-constructive - demon-
strating existence of an object without actually constructing the object
explicitly. This problem is not unique to the axiom of choice - it already
appears for instance in Lemma 3.1.6 - but the objects shown to exist
using the axiom of choice tend to be rather extreme in their level of
non-constructiveness. However, as long as one is aware of the distinc-
tion between a non-constructive existence statement, and a constructive
existence statement (with the latter being preferable, but not strictly
necessary in many cases), there is no diï¬ƒculty here, except perhaps on
a philosophical level.
Remark 8.4.4. There are many equivalent formulations of the axiom
of choice; we give some of these in the exercises below.
In analysis one often does not need the full power of the axiom of
choice.
Instead, one often only needs the axiom of countable choice,
which is the same as the axiom of choice but with the index set I re-
stricted to be at most countable.
We give a typical example of this
below.

8.5
Ordered sets
The axiom of choice is intimately connected to the theory of ordered sets.
There are actually many types of ordered sets; we will concern ourselves
with three such types, the partially ordered sets, the totally ordered sets,
and the well-ordered sets.
Deï¬nition 8.5.1 (Partially ordered sets). A partially ordered set (or
poset) is a set X, together1 with a relation â‰¤X on X (thus for any two
objects x, y âˆˆX, the statement x â‰¤X y is either a true statement or
a false statement). Furthermore, this relation is assumed to obey the
following three properties:
â€¢ (Reï¬‚exivity) For any x âˆˆX, we have x â‰¤X x.
â€¢ (Anti-symmetry) If x, y âˆˆX are such that x â‰¤X y and y â‰¤X x,
then x = y.
â€¢ (Transitivity) If x, y, z âˆˆX are such that x â‰¤X y and y â‰¤X z,
then x â‰¤X z.
We refer to â‰¤X as the ordering relation. In most situations it is under-
stood what the set X is from context, and in those cases we shall simply
write â‰¤instead of â‰¤X. We write x <X y (or x < y for short) if x â‰¤X y
and x Ì¸= y.
Examples 8.5.2. The natural numbers N together with the usual less-
than-or-equal-to relation â‰¤(as deï¬ned in Deï¬nition 2.2.11) forms a par-
tially ordered set, by Proposition 2.2.12. Similar arguments (using the
1Strictly speaking, a partially ordered set is not a set X, but rather a pair (X, â‰¤X).
But in many cases the ordering â‰¤X will be clear from context, and so we shall refer
to X itself as the partially ordered set even though this is technically incorrect.

8.5.
Ordered sets
203
appropriate deï¬nitions and propositions) show that the integers Z, the
rationals Q, the reals R, and the extended reals Râˆ—are also partially
ordered sets. Meanwhile, if X is any collection of sets, and one uses the
relation of is-a-subset-of âŠ†(as deï¬ned in Deï¬nition 3.1.15) for the order-
ing relation â‰¤X, then X is also partially ordered (Proposition 3.1.18).
Note that it is certainly possible to give these sets a diï¬€erent partial
ordering than the standard one; see for instance Exercise 8.5.3.
Deï¬nition 8.5.3 (Totally ordered set). Let X be a partially ordered
set with some order relation â‰¤X. A subset Y of X is said to be totally
ordered if, given any two y, yâ€² âˆˆY , we either have y â‰¤X yâ€² or yâ€² â‰¤X y
(or both). If X itself is totally ordered, we say that X is a totally ordered

On the other hand, a collection of sets with the âŠ†

For instance, if X is the set
{{1, 2}, {2}, {2, 3}, {2, 3, 4}, {5}}, ordered by the set inclusion relation
âŠ†, then the elements {1, 2} and {2, 3} of X are not comparable to each
other (i.e., {1, 2} Ì¸âŠ†{2, 3} and {2, 3} Ì¸âŠ†{1, 2}).
Deï¬nition 8.5.5 (Maximal and minimal elements). Let X be a par-
tially ordered set, and let Y be a subset of X. We say that y is a minimal
element of Y if y âˆˆY and there is no element yâ€² âˆˆY such that yâ€² < y.
We say that y is a maximal element of Y if y âˆˆY and there is no
element yâ€² âˆˆY such that y < yâ€².
Example 8.5.6. Using the set X from the previous example, {2} is a
minimal element, {1, 2} and {2, 3, 4} are maximal elements, {5} is both
a minimal and a maximal element, and {2, 3} is neither a minimal nor
a maximal element. This example shows that a partially ordered set
can have multiple maxima and minima; however, a totally ordered set
cannot (Exercise 8.5.7).
Example 8.5.7. The natural numbers N (ordered by â‰¤) has a minimal
element, namely 0, but no maximal element. The set of integers Z has
no maximal and no minimal element.

8.
Inï¬nite sets
Deï¬nition 8.5.8 (Well-ordered sets). Let X be a partially ordered set,
and let Y be a totally ordered subset of X. We say that Y is well-ordered
if every non-empty subset of Y has a minimal element min(Y ).
Examples 8.5.9. The natural numbers N are well-ordered by Propo-
sition 8.1.4.
However, the integers Z, the rationals Q, and the real
numbers R are not (see Exercise 8.1.2). Every ï¬nite totally ordered set
is well-ordered (Exercise 8.5.8). Every subset of a well-ordered set is
again well-ordered (why?).
One advantage of well-ordered sets is that they automatically obey
a principle of strong induction (cf. Proposition 2.2.14):
Proposition 8.5.10 (Principle of strong induction). Let X be a well-
ordered set with an ordering relation â‰¤, and let P(n) be a property per-
taining to an element n âˆˆX (i.e., for each n âˆˆX, P(n) is either a true
statement or a false statement). Suppose that for every n âˆˆX, we have
the following implication: if P(m) is true for all m âˆˆX with m <X n,
then P(n) is also true. Prove that P(n) is true for all n âˆˆX.
Remark 8.5.11. It may seem strange that there is no â€œbaseâ€ case
in strong induction, corresponding to the hypothesis P(0) in Axiom
2.5. However, such a base case is automatically included in the strong
induction hypothesis. Indeed, if 0 is the minimal element of X, then by
specializing the hypothesis â€œif P(m) is true for all m âˆˆX with m <X n,
then P(n) is also trueâ€ to the n = 0 case, we automatically obtain that
P(0) is true. (Why?)
Proof. See Exercise 8.5.10.
So far we have not seen the axiom of choice play any rË†ole. This will
come in once we introduce the notion of an upper bound and a strict
upper bound.
Deï¬nition 8.5.12 (Upper bounds and strict upper bounds). Let X be
a partially ordered set with ordering relation â‰¤, and let Y be a subset
of X. If x âˆˆX, we say that x is an upper bound for Y iï¬€y â‰¤x for all
y âˆˆY . If in addition x Ì¸âˆˆY , we say that x is a strict upper bound for
Y . Equivalently, x is a strict upper bound for Y iï¬€y < x for all y âˆˆY .
(Why is this equivalent?)
Example 8.5.13. Let us work in the real number system R with the
usual ordering â‰¤. Then 2 is an upper bound for the set {x âˆˆR : 1 â‰¤

x â‰¤2} but is not a strict upper bound. The number 3, on the other
hand, is a strict upper bound for this set.
Lemma 8.5.14. Let X be a partially ordered set with ordering relation
â‰¤, and let x0 be an element of X. Then there is a well-ordered subset
Y of X which has x0 as its minimal element, and which has no strict
upper bound.
Proof. The intuition behind this lemma is that one is trying to perform
the following algorithm: we initalize Y := {x0}. If Y has no strict upper
bound, then we are done; otherwise, we choose a strict upper bound and
add it to Y . Then we look again to see if Y has a strict upper bound
or not. If not, we are done; otherwise we choose another strict upper
bound and add it to Y . We continue this algorithm â€œinï¬nitely oftenâ€
until we exhaust all the strict upper bounds; the axiom of choice comes
in because inï¬nitely many choices are involved. This is however not a
rigorous proof because it is quite diï¬ƒcult to precisely pin down what it
means to perform an algorithm â€œinï¬nitely oftenâ€. Instead, what we will
do is that we will isolate a collection of â€œpartially completedâ€ sets Y ,
which we shall call good sets, and then take the union of all these good
sets to obtain a â€œcompletedâ€ object Yâˆwhich will indeed have no strict
upper bound.
We now begin the rigorous proof. Suppose for sake of contradiction
that every well-ordered subset Y of X which has x0 as its minimal
element has at least one strict upper bound. Using the axiom of choice
(in the form of Proposition 8.4.7), we can thus assign a strict upper
bound s(Y ) âˆˆX to each well-ordered subset Y of X which has x0 as its
minimal element.
Let us deï¬ne a special class of subsets Y of X. We say that a subset
Y of X is good iï¬€it is well-ordered, contains x0 as its minimal element,
and obeys the property that
x = s({y âˆˆY : y < x}) for all x âˆˆY \{x0}.
Note that if x âˆˆY \{x0} then the set {y âˆˆY : y < x} is a subset of
X which is well-ordered and contains x0 as its minimal element. Let
Î© := {Y âŠ†X : Y is good} be the collection of all good subsets of X.
This collection is not empty, since the subset {x0} of X is clearly good
(why?).
We make the following important observation: if Y and Y â€² are two
good subsets of X, then every element of Y â€²\Y is a strict upper bound for

206
8.
Inï¬nite sets
Y , and every element of Y \Y â€² is a strict upper bound for Y â€². (Exercise
8.5.13). In particular, given any two good sets Y and Y â€², at least one of
Y â€²\Y and Y \Y â€² must be empty (since they are both strict upper bounds
of each other). In other words, Î© is totally ordered by set inclusion: given
any two good sets Y and Y â€², either Y âŠ†Y â€² or Y â€² âŠ†Y .
Let Yâˆ:=  Î©, i.e., Yâˆis the set of all elements of X which belong
to at least one good subset of X. Clearly x0 âˆˆYâˆ. Also, since each
good subset of X has x0 as its minimal element, the set Yâˆalso has x0
as its minimal element (why?).
Next, we show that Yâˆis totally ordered. Let x, xâ€² be two elements
of Yâˆ. By deï¬nition of Yâˆ, we know that x lies in some good set Y
and xâ€² lies in some good set Y â€². But since Î© is totally ordered, one of
these good sets contains the other. Thus x, xâ€² are contained in a single
good set (either Y or Y â€²); since good sets are totally ordered, we thus

Next, we show that Yâˆis well-ordered. Let A be any non-empty
subset of Yâˆ. Then we can pick an element a âˆˆA, which then lies in
Yâˆ. Therefore there is a good set Y such that a âˆˆY . Then A âˆ©Y is a

a minimal element, call it b. Now recall that for any other good set Y â€²,
every element of Y â€²\Y is a strict upper bound for Y , and in particular is
larger than b. Since b is a minimal element of A âˆ©Y , this implies that b
is also a minimal element of A âˆ©Y â€² for any good set Y â€² with A âˆ©Y â€² Ì¸= âˆ…
(why?). Since every element of A belongs to Yâˆand hence belongs to
at least one good set Y â€², we thus see that b is a minimal element of A.
Thus Yâˆis well-ordered as claimed.
Since Yâˆis well-ordered with x0 as its minimal element, it has a
strict upper bound s(Yâˆ). But then Yâˆâˆª{s(Yâˆ)} is well-ordered (why?
see Exercise 8.5.11) and has x0 as its minimal element (why?). Thus
this set is good, and must therefore be contained in Yâˆ. But this is a
contradiction since s(Yâˆ) is a strict upper bound for Yâˆ. Thus we have
constructed a set with no strict upper bound, as desired.
The above lemma has the following important consequence:
Lemma 8.5.15 (Zornâ€™s lemma). Let X be a non-empty partially ordered
set, with the property that every totally ordered subset Y of X has an
upper bound. Then X contains at least one maximal element.
Proof. See Exercise 8.5.14.

Chapter 9
Continuous functions on R
In previous chapters we have been focusing primarily on sequences. A
sequence (an)âˆ
n=0 can be viewed as a function from N to R, i.e., an
object which assigns a real number an to each natural number n. We
then did various things with these functions from N to R, such as take
their limit at inï¬nity (if the function was convergent), or form suprema,
inï¬ma, etc., or computed the sum of all the elements in the sequence
(again, assuming the series was convergent).
Now we will look at functions not on the natural numbers N, which
are â€œdiscreteâ€, but instead look at functions on a continuum1 such as
the real line R, or perhaps on an interval such as {x âˆˆR : a â‰¤x â‰¤b}.
Eventually we will perform a number of operations on these functions,
including taking limits, computing derivatives, and evaluating integrals.
In this chapter we will focus primarily on limits of functions, and on the
closely related concept of a continuous function.
Before we discuss functions, though, we must ï¬rst set out some no-
tation for subsets of the real line.
9.1
Subsets of the real line
Very often in analysis we do not work on the whole real line R, but
on certain subsets of the real line, such as the positive real axis {x âˆˆ
R : x > 0}. Also, we occasionally work with the extended real line Râˆ—
deï¬ned in Section 6.2, or in subsets of that extended real line.
1We will not rigorously deï¬ne the notion of a discrete set or a continuum in this
text, but roughly speaking a set is discrete if each element is separated from the rest
of the set by some non-zero distance, whereas a set is a continuum if it is connected
and contains no â€œholesâ€.

DOI 10.1007/978-981-10-1789-6_9

9.
Continuous functions on R
There are of course inï¬nitely many subsets of the real line; indeed,
Cantorâ€™s theorem (Theorem 8.3.1; see also Exercise 8.3.4) shows that
there are even more such sets than there are real numbers. However,
there are certain special subsets of the real line (and the extended real
line) which arise quite often. One such family of sets are the intervals.
Deï¬nition 9.1.1 (Intervals). Let a, b âˆˆRâˆ—be extended real numbers.
We deï¬ne the closed interval [a, b] by
[a, b] := {x âˆˆRâˆ—: a â‰¤x â‰¤b},
the half-open intervals [a, b) and (a, b] by
[a, b) := {x âˆˆRâˆ—: a â‰¤x < b};
(a, b] := {x âˆˆRâˆ—: a < x â‰¤b},
and the open intervals (a, b) by
(a, b) := {x âˆˆRâˆ—: a < x < b}.
We call a the left endpoint of these intervals, and b the right endpoint.
Remark 9.1.2. Once again, we are overloading the parenthesis no-
tation; for instance, we are now using (2, 3) to denote both an open
interval from 2 to 3, as well as an ordered pair in the Cartesian plane
R2 := R Ã— R. This can cause some genuine ambiguity, but the reader
should still be able to resolve which meaning of the parentheses is in-
tended from context. In some texts, this issue is resolved by using re-
versed brackets instead of parenthesis, thus for instance [a, b) would now
be [a, b[, (a, b] would be ]a, b], and (a, b) would be ]a, b[.
Examples 9.1.3. If a and b are real numbers (i.e., not equal to +âˆ
or âˆ’âˆ) then all of the above intervals are subsets of the real line, for
instance [2, 3) = {x âˆˆR : 2 â‰¤x < 3}. The positive real axis {x âˆˆ
R : x > 0} is the open interval (0, +âˆ), while the non-negative real
axis {x âˆˆR : x â‰¥0} is the half-open interval [0, +âˆ). Similarly, the
negative real axis {x âˆˆR : x < 0} is (âˆ’âˆ, 0), and the non-positive real
axis {x âˆˆR : x â‰¤0} is (âˆ’âˆ, 0]. Finally, the real line R itself is the open
interval (âˆ’âˆ, +âˆ), while the extended real line Râˆ—is the closed interval
[âˆ’âˆ, +âˆ]. We sometimes refer to an interval in which one endpoint is
inï¬nite (either +âˆor âˆ’âˆ) as half-inï¬nite intervals, and intervals in
which both endpoints are inï¬nite as doubly-inï¬nite intervals; all other
intervals are bounded intervals. Thus [2, 3) is a bounded interval, the
positive and negative real axes are half-inï¬nite intervals, and R and Râˆ—
are inï¬nite intervals.

9.1.
Subsets of the real line
213
Example 9.1.4. If a > b then all four of the intervals [a, b], [a, b), (a, b],
and (a, b) are the empty set (why?). If a = b, then the three intervals
[a, b), (a, b], and (a, b) are the empty set, while [a, b] is just the singleton
set {a} (why?). Because of this, we call these intervals degenerate; most
(but not all) of our analysis will be restricted to non-degenerate intervals.
Of course intervals are not the only interesting subsets of the real
line.
Other important examples include the natural numbers N, the
integers Z, and the rationals Q. One can form additional sets using
such operations as union and intersection (see Section 3.1), for instance
one could have a disconnected union of two intervals such as (1, 2)âˆª[3, 4],
or one could consider the set [âˆ’1, 1] âˆ©Q of rational numbers between
âˆ’1 and 1 inclusive. Clearly there are inï¬nitely many possibilities of sets
one could create by such operations.
Just as sequences of real numbers have limit points, sets of real
numbers have adherent points, which we now deï¬ne.
Deï¬nition 9.1.5 (Îµ-adherent points). Let X be a subset of R, let Îµ > 0,
and let x âˆˆR. We say that x is Îµ-adherent to X iï¬€there exists a y âˆˆX
which is Îµ-close to x (i.e., |x âˆ’y| â‰¤Îµ).
Remark 9.1.6. The terminology â€œÎµ-adherentâ€ is not standard in the
literature. However, we shall shortly use it to deï¬ne the notion of an
adherent point, which is standard.
Example 9.1.7. The point 1.1 is 0.5-adherent to the open interval
(0, 1), but is not 0.1-adherent to this interval (why?). The point 1.1 is
0.5-adherent to the ï¬nite set {1, 2, 3}. The point 1 is 0.5-adherent to
{1, 2, 3} (why?).
Deï¬nition 9.1.8 (Adherent points). Let X be a subset of R, and let
x âˆˆR. We say that x is an adherent point of X iï¬€it is Îµ-adherent to
X for every Îµ > 0.
Example 9.1.9. The number 1 is Îµ-adherent to the open interval (0, 1)
for every Îµ > 0 (why?), and is thus an adherent point of (0, 1). The
point 0.5 is similarly an adherent point of (0, 1). However, the number
2 is not 0.5-adherent (for instance) to (0, 1), and is thus not an adherent
point to (0, 1).
Deï¬nition 9.1.10 (Closure). Let X be a subset of R. The closure of
X, sometimes denoted X is deï¬ned to be the set of all the adherent
points of X.

Lemma 9.1.11 (Elementary properties of closures). Let X and Y be

X âˆ©Y . If X âŠ†Y , then X âŠ†Y .
Proof. See Exercise 9.1.2.
We now compute some closures.
Lemma 9.1.12 (Closures of intervals). Let a < b be real numbers, and
let I be any one of the four intervals (a, b), (a, b], [a, b), or [a, b]. Then
the closure of I is [a, b]. Similarly, the closure of (a, âˆ) or [a, âˆ) is
[a, âˆ), while the closure of (âˆ’âˆ, a) or (âˆ’âˆ, a] is (âˆ’âˆ, a]. Finally, the
closure of (âˆ’âˆ, âˆ) is (âˆ’âˆ, âˆ).
Proof. We will just show one of these facts, namely that the closure
of (a, b) is [a, b]; the other results are proven similarly (or one can use
Exercise 9.1.1).
First let us show that every element of [a, b] is adherent to (a, b). Let
x âˆˆ[a, b]. If x âˆˆ(a, b) then it is deï¬nitely adherent to (a, b). If x = b
then x is also adherent to (a, b) (why?). Similarly when x = a. Thus
every point in [a, b] is adherent to (a, b).
Now we show that every point x that is adherent to (a, b) lies in [a, b].
Suppose for sake of contradiction that x does not lie in [a, b], then either
x > b or x < a. If x > b then x is not (x âˆ’b)-adherent to (a, b) (why?),
and is hence not an adherent point to (a, b). Similarly, if x < a, then x
is not (a âˆ’x)-adherent to (a âˆ’b), and is hence not an adherent point to
(a, b). This contradiction shows that x is in fact in [a, b] as claimed.
Lemma 9.1.13. The closure of N is N. The closure of Z is Z. The
closure of Q is R, and the closure of R is R. The closure of the empty
set âˆ…is âˆ….
Proof. See Exercise 9.1.3.

A basic property of closed and bounded sets is the following.

9.2
The algebra of real-valued functions
You are familiar with many functions f : R â†’R from the real line to the
real line. Some examples are: f(x) := x2 + 3x + 5; f(x) := 2x/(x2 + 1);

218
9.
Continuous functions on R
f(x) := sin(x) exp(x) (we will deï¬ne sin and exp formally in Chapter
11.20). These are functions from R to R since to every real number x
they assign a single real number f(x). We can also consider more exotic
functions, e.g.
f(x) :=
! 1
if x âˆˆQ
0
if x Ì¸âˆˆQ.
This function is not algebraic (i.e., it cannot be expressed in terms of
x purely by using the standard algebraic operations of +, âˆ’, Ã—, /, âˆš,
etc.; we will not need this notion in this text), but it is still a function
from R to R, because it still assigns a real number f(x) to each x âˆˆR.
We can take any one of the previous functions f : R â†’R deï¬ned on
all of R, and restrict the domain to a smaller set X âŠ†R, creating a new
function, sometimes called f|X, from X to R. This is the same function
as the original function f, but is only deï¬ned on a smaller domain. (Thus
f|X(x) := f(x) when x âˆˆX, and f|X(x) is undeï¬ned when x Ì¸âˆˆX.)
For instance, we can restrict the function f(x) := x2, which is initially
deï¬ned from R to R, to the interval [1, 2], thus creating a new function
f|[1,2] : [1, 2] â†’R, which is deï¬ned as f|[1,2](x) = x2 when x âˆˆ[1, 2] but
is undeï¬ned elsewhere.
One could also restrict the range from R to some smaller subset Y
of R, provided of course that all the values of f(x) lie inside Y . For
instance, the function f : R â†’R deï¬ned by f(x) := x2 could also be
thought of as a function from R to [0, âˆ), instead of a function from
R to R. Formally, these two functions are diï¬€erent functions, but the
distinction between them is so minor that we shall often be careless
about the range of a function in our discussion.
Strictly speaking, there is a distinction between a function f, and
its value f(x) at a point x. f is a function; but f(x) is a number (which
depends on some free variable x). This distinction is rather subtle and
we will not stress it too much, but there are times when one has to
distinguish between the two. For instance, if f : R â†’R is the function
f(x) := x2, and g := f|[1,2] is the restriction of f to the interval [1, 2],
then f and g both perform the operation of squaring, i.e., f(x) = x2
and g(x) = x2, but the two functions f and g are not considered the
same function, f Ì¸= g, because they have diï¬€erent domains. Despite this
distinction, we shall often be careless, and say things like â€œconsider the
function x2 + 2x + 3â€ when really we should be saying â€œconsider the
function f : R â†’R deï¬ned by f(x) := x2 + 2x + 3â€. (This distinction
makes more of a diï¬€erence when we start doing things like diï¬€erentiation.

9.2.
The algebra of real-valued functions
219
For instance, if f : R â†’R is the function f(x) = x2, then of course
f(3) = 9, but the derivative of f at 3 is 6, whereas the derivative of 9 is
of course 0, so we cannot simply â€œdiï¬€erentiate both sidesâ€ of f(3) = 9
and conclude that 6 = 0.)
If X is a subset of R, and f : X â†’R is a function, we can form
the graph {(x, f(x)) : x âˆˆX} of the function f; this is a subset of
X Ã— R, and hence a subset of the Euclidean plane R2 = R Ã— R. One
can certainly study a function through its graph, by using the geometry
of the plane R2 (e.g., employing such concepts as tangent lines, area,
and so forth). We however will pursue a more â€œanalyticâ€ approach, in
which we rely instead on the properties of the real numbers to analyze
these functions. The two approaches are complementary; the geometric
approach oï¬€ers more visual intuition, while the analytic approach oï¬€ers
rigour and precision.
Both the geometric intuition and the analytic
formalism become useful when extending analysis of functions of one
variable to functions of many variables (or possibly even inï¬nitely many
variables).
Just as numbers can be manipulated arithmetically, so can functions:
the sum of two functions is a function, the product of two functions is a
function, and so forth.
Deï¬nition 9.2.1 (Arithmetic operations on functions). Given two func-
tions f : X â†’R and g : X â†’R, we can deï¬ne their sum f +g : X â†’R
by the formula
(f + g)(x) := f(x) + g(x),
their diï¬€erence f âˆ’g : X â†’R by the formula
(f âˆ’g)(x) := f(x) âˆ’g(x),
their maximum max(f, g) : X â†’R by
max(f, g)(x) := max(f(x), g(x)),
their minimum min(f, g) : X â†’R by
min(f, g)(x) := min(f(x), g(x)),
their product fg : X â†’R (or f Â· g : X â†’R) by the formula
(fg)(x) := f(x)g(x),

and (provided that g(x) Ì¸= 0 for all x âˆˆX) the quotient f/g : X â†’R
by the formula
(f/g)(x) := f(x)/g(x).
Finally, if c is a real number, we can deï¬ne the function cf : X â†’R (or
c Â· f : X â†’R) by the formula

Example 9.2.2. If f : R â†’R is the function f(x) := x2, and g :

Proposition 9.3.9 has some immediate corollaries. For instance, we
now know that a function can have at most one limit at each point:
Corollary 9.3.13. Let X be a subset of R, let E be a subset of X, let
x0 be an adherent point of E, and let f : X â†’R be a function. Then f
can have at most one limit at x0 in E.

But this contradicts the uniqueness of limits of sequences (Proposition

If f converges to L at x0 in X, and Y is any subset of X such that

9.4
Continuous functions
We now introduce one of the most fundamental notions in the theory of
functions - that of continuity.
Deï¬nition 9.4.1 (Continuity). Let X be a subset of R, and let f :
X â†’R be a function. Let x0 be an element of X. We say that f is
continuous at x0 iï¬€we have
lim
xâ†’x0;xâˆˆX f(x) = f(x0);
in other words, the limit of f(x) as x converges to x0 in X exists and is
equal to f(x0). We say that f is continuous on X (or simply continuous)
iï¬€f is continuous at x0 for every x0 âˆˆX. We say that f is discontinuous
at x0 iï¬€it is not continuous at x0.
Example 9.4.2. Let c be a real number, and let f : R â†’R be the

lim

thus f is continuous at every point x0 âˆˆR, or in other words f is
continuous on R.

There are several ways to phrase the statement that â€œf is continuous
at x0â€:
Proposition 9.4.7 (Equivalent formulations of continuity). Let X be a
subset of R, let f : X â†’R be a function, and let x0 be an element of
X. Then the following four statements are logically equivalent:
(a) f is continuous at x0.
(b) For every sequence (an)âˆ
n=0 consisting of elements of X with
limnâ†’âˆan = x0, we have limnâ†’âˆf(an) = f(x0).
(c) For every Îµ > 0, there exists a Î´ > 0 such that |f(x) âˆ’f(x0)| < Îµ
for all x âˆˆX with |x âˆ’x0| < Î´.
(d) For every Îµ > 0, there exists a Î´ > 0 such that |f(x) âˆ’f(x0)| â‰¤Îµ
for all x âˆˆX with |x âˆ’x0| â‰¤Î´.
Proof. See Exercise 9.4.1.
Remark 9.4.8. A particularly useful consequence of Proposition 9.4.7
is the following: if f is continuous at x0, and an â†’x0 as n â†’âˆ, then
f(an) â†’f(x0) as n â†’âˆ(provided that all the elements of the sequence
(an)âˆ
n=0 lie in the domain of f, of course). Thus continuous functions
are very useful in computing limits.
The limit laws in Proposition 9.3.14, combined with the deï¬nition of
continuity in Deï¬nition 9.4.1, immediately imply
Proposition 9.4.9 (Arithmetic preserves continuity). Let X be a subset
of R, and let f : X â†’R and g : X â†’R be functions. Let x0 âˆˆX.
Then if f and g are both continuous at x0, then the functions f + g,
f âˆ’g, max(f, g), min(f, g) and fg are also continuous at x0. If g is
non-zero on X, then f/g is also continuous at x0.
In particular, the sum, diï¬€erence, maximum, minimum, and product
of continuous functions are continuous; and the quotient of two continu-
ous functions is continuous as long as the denominator does not become
zero.
One can use Proposition 9.4.9 to show that a lot of functions are
continuous.
For instance, just by starting from the fact that con-
stant functions are continuous, and the identity function f(x) = x is
continuous (Exercise 9.4.2), one can show that the function g(x) :=
max(x3 + 4x2 + x + 5, x4 âˆ’x3)/(x2 âˆ’4), for instance, is continuous at
every point of R except the two points x = +2, x = âˆ’2 where the
denominator vanishes.

Some other examples of continuous functions are given below.
Proposition 9.4.10 (Exponentiation is continuous, I). Let a > 0 be a
positive real number. Then the function f : R â†’R deï¬ned by f(x) := ax
is continuous.
Proof. See Exercise 9.4.3.
Proposition 9.4.11 (Exponentiation is continuous, II). Let p be a real
number. Then the function f : (0, âˆ) â†’R deï¬ned by f(x) := xp is

Proof. See Exercise 9.4.4.
There is a stronger statement than Propositions 9.4.10, 9.4.11,
namely that exponentiation is jointly continuous in both the exponent
and the base, but this is harder to show; see Exercise 11.25.10.
Proposition 9.4.12 (Absolute value is continuous). The function f :
R â†’R deï¬ned by f(x) := |x| is continuous.
Proof. This follows since |x| = max(x, âˆ’x) and the functions x, âˆ’x are
already continuous.
The class of continuous functions is not only closed under addition,
subtraction, multiplication, and division, but is also closed under com-
position:
Proposition 9.4.13 (Composition preserves continuity). Let X and Y
be subsets of R, and let f : X â†’Y and g : Y â†’R be functions. Let x0
be a point in X. If f is continuous at x0, and g is continuous at f(x0),
then the composition g â—¦f : X â†’R is continuous at x0.
Proof. See Exercise 9.4.5.
Example 9.4.14. Since the function f(x) := 3x+1 is continuous on all
of R, and the function g(x) := 5x is continuous on all of R, the function
g â—¦f(x) = 53x+1 is continuous on all of R. By several applications of the
above propositions, one can show that far more complicated functions,
e.g., h(x) := |x2 âˆ’8x + 7|
âˆš
2/(x2 + 1), are also continuous. (Why is this
function continuous?) There are still a few functions though that are
not yet easy to test for continuity, such as k(x) := xx; this function can
be dealt with more easily once we have the machinery of logarithms,
which we will see in Section 11.25.

such that |f(x) âˆ’L| < Îµ for all x âˆˆX âˆ©(x0, âˆ) for which |x âˆ’x0| < Î´+.
From (9.2) we similarly know that there exists a Î´âˆ’> 0 such that
|f(x) âˆ’L| < Îµ for all x âˆˆX âˆ©(âˆ’âˆ, x0) for which |x âˆ’x0| < Î´âˆ’. Now
let Î´ := min(Î´âˆ’, Î´+); then Î´ > 0 (why?), and suppose that x âˆˆX is
such that |x âˆ’x0| < Î´. Then there are three cases: x > x0, x = x0, and
x < x0, but in all three cases we know that |f(x) âˆ’L| < Îµ. (Why? the
reason is diï¬€erent in each of the three cases.) By Proposition 9.4.7 we
thus have that f is continuous at x0, as desired.
As we saw with the signum function in Example 9.3.16, it is possible
for the left and right limits f(x0âˆ’), f(x0+) of a function f at a point x0
to both exist, but not be equal to each other; when this happens, we say
that f has a jump discontinuity at x0. Thus, for instance, the signum
function has a jump discontinuity at zero. Also, it is possible for the left
and right limits f(x0âˆ’), f(x0+) to exist and be equal each other, but
not be equal to f(x0); when this happens we say that f has a removable
discontinuity (or removable singularity) at x0. For instance, if we take
f : R â†’R to be the function
f(x) :=
! 1
if x = 0
0
if x Ì¸= 0,
then f(0+) and f(0âˆ’) both exist and equal 0 (why?), but f(0) equals
1; thus f has a removable discontinuity at 0.

Remark 9.5.4. Jump discontinuities and removable discontinuities are
not the only way a function can be discontinuous. Another way is for a
function to go to inï¬nity at the discontinuity: for instance, the function
f : Râˆ’{0} â†’R deï¬ned by f(x) := 1/x has a discontinuity at 0 which is
neither a jump discontinuity or a removable singularity; informally, f(x)
converges to +âˆwhen x approaches 0 from the right, and converges to
âˆ’âˆwhen x approaches 0 from the left. These types of singularities are
sometimes known as asymptotic discontinuities. There are also oscilla-
tory discontinuities, where the function remains bounded but still does
not have a limit near x0. For instance, the function f : R â†’R deï¬ned
by
f(x) :=
! 1
if x âˆˆQ
0
if x Ì¸âˆˆQ
has an oscillatory discontinuity at 0 (and in fact at any other real number
also). This is because the function does not have left or right limits at
0, despite the fact that the function is bounded.
The study of discontinuities (also called singularities) continues fur-
ther, but is beyond the scope of this text. For instance, singularities
play a key rË†ole in complex analysis.

9.6
The maximum principle
In the previous two sections we saw that a large number of functions
were continuous, though certainly not all functions were continuous.
We now show that continuous functions enjoy a number of other useful
properties, especially if their domain is a closed interval.
It is here
that we shall begin exploiting the full power of the Heine-Borel theorem
(Theorem 9.1.24).
Deï¬nition 9.6.1. Let X be a subset of R, and let f : X â†’R be a
function. We say that f is bounded from above if there exists a real
number M such that f(x) â‰¤M for all x âˆˆX. We say that f is bounded

9.6.
The maximum principle
235
from below if there exists a real number M such that f(x) â‰¥âˆ’M for all
x âˆˆX. We say that f is bounded if there exists a real number M such
that |f(x)| â‰¤M for all x âˆˆX.
Remark 9.6.2. A function is bounded if and only if it is bounded both
from above and below. (Why? Note that one part of the â€œif and only
ifâ€ is slightly trickier than the other.) Also, a function f : X â†’R is
bounded if and only if its image f(X) is a bounded set in the sense of
Deï¬nition 9.1.22 (why?).
Not all continuous functions are bounded. For instance, the func-
tion f(x) := x on the domain R is continuous but unbounded (why?),
although it is bounded on some smaller domains, such as [1, 2]. The
function f(x) := 1/x is continuous but unbounded on (0, 1) (why?),
though it is continuous and bounded on [1, 2] (why?). However, if the
domain of the continuous function is a closed and bounded interval, then
we do have boundedness:
Lemma 9.6.3. Let a < b be real numbers, and let f : [a, b] â†’R be a
function continuous on [a, b]. Then f is a bounded function.
Proof. Suppose for sake of contradiction that f is not bounded. Thus

We now improve Lemma 9.6.3 to say something more.
Deï¬nition 9.6.5 (Maxima and minima). Let f : X â†’R be a function,
and let x0 âˆˆX. We say that f attains its maximum at x0 if we have
f(x0) â‰¥f(x) for all x âˆˆX (i.e., the value of f at the point x0 is larger

Remark 9.6.6. If a function attains its maximum somewhere, then it
must be bounded from above (why?). Similarly if it attains its minimum
somewhere, then it must be bounded from below. These notions of max-
ima and minima are global; local versions will be deï¬ned in Deï¬nition
10.2.1.

used to denote either a maximum or a minimum.

We now know that on a closed interval, every continuous function is
bounded and attains its maximum at least once and minimum at least
once. The same is not true for open or inï¬nite intervals; see Exercise

Remark 9.6.9. You may encounter a rather diï¬€erent â€œmaximum prin-
cipleâ€ in complex analysis or partial diï¬€erential equations, involving
analytic functions and harmonic functions respectively, instead of con-
tinuous functions. Those maximum principles are not directly related
to this one (though they are also concerned with whether maxima exist,
and where the maxima are located).

9.7
The intermediate value theorem
We have just shown that a continuous function attains both its maximum
value and its minimum value. We now show that f also attains every
value in between. To do this, we ï¬rst prove a very intuitive theorem:
Theorem 9.7.1 (Intermediate value theorem). Let a < b, and let f :
[a, b] â†’R be a continuous function on [a, b]. Let y be a real number

The intermediate value theorem says that if f takes the values f(a)
and f(b), then it must also take all the values in between. Note that if
f is not assumed to be continuous, then the intermediate value theorem
no longer applies. For instance, if f : [âˆ’1, 1] â†’R is the function
f(x) :=
! âˆ’1
if x â‰¤0
1
if x > 0
then f(âˆ’1) = âˆ’1, and f(1) = 1, but there is no c âˆˆ[âˆ’1, 1] for which
f(c) = 0. Thus if a function is discontinuous, it can â€œjumpâ€ past inter-
mediate values; however continuous functions cannot do so.
Remark 9.7.2. A continuous function may take an intermediate value
multiple times. For instance, if f : [âˆ’2, 2] â†’R is the function f(x) :=
x3 âˆ’x, then f(âˆ’2) = âˆ’6 and f(2) = 6, so we know that there exists
a c âˆˆ[âˆ’2, 2] for which f(c) = 0. In fact, in this case there exists three
such values of c: we have f(âˆ’1) = f(0) = f(1) = 0.
Remark 9.7.3. The intermediate value theorem gives another way to
show that one can take nth roots of a number. For instance, to construct
the square root of 2, consider the function f : [0, 2] â†’R deï¬ned by
f(x) = x2. This function is continuous, with f(0) = 0 and f(2) = 4.
Thus there exists a c âˆˆ[0, 2] such that f(c) = 2, i.e., c2 = 2. (This
argument does not show that there is just one square root of 2, but it
does prove that there is at least one square root of 2.)
Corollary 9.7.4 (Images of continuous functions). Let a < b, and let
f : [a, b] â†’R be a continuous function on [a, b]. Let M := supxâˆˆ[a,b] f(x)
be the maximum value of f, and let m := infxâˆˆ[a,b] f(x) be the minimum
value. Let y be a real number between m and M (i.e., m â‰¤y â‰¤M).
Then there exists a c âˆˆ[a, b] such that f(c) = y. Furthermore, we have
f([a, b]) = [m, M].
Proof. See Exercise 9.7.1.

9.8
Monotonic functions
We now discuss a class of functions which is distinct from the class of
continuous functions, but has somewhat similar properties: the class of
monotone (or monotonic) functions.
Deï¬nition 9.8.1 (Monotonic functions). Let X be a subset of R, and
let f : X â†’R be a function. We say that f is monotone increasing iï¬€
f(y) â‰¥f(x) whenever x, y âˆˆX and y > x. We say that f is strictly
monotone increasing iï¬€f(y) > f(x) whenever x, y âˆˆX and y > x.
Similarly, we say f is monotone decreasing iï¬€f(y) â‰¤f(x) whenever
x, y âˆˆX and y > x, and strictly monotone decreasing iï¬€f(y) < f(x)
whenever x, y âˆˆX and y > x.
We say that f is monotone if it is
monotone increasing or monotone decreasing, and strictly monotone if
it is strictly monotone increasing or strictly monotone decreasing.
Examples 9.8.2. The function f(x) := x2, when restricted to the do-
main [0, âˆ), is strictly monotone increasing (why?), but when restricted
instead to the domain (âˆ’âˆ, 0], is strictly monotone decreasing (why?).
Thus the function is strictly monotone on both (âˆ’âˆ, 0] and [0, âˆ), but
is not strictly monotone (or monotone) on the full real line (âˆ’âˆ, âˆ).
Note that if a function is strictly monotone on a domain X, it is au-
tomatically monotone as well on the same domain X.
The constant
function f(x) := 6, when restricted to an arbitrary domain X âŠ†R, is
both monotone increasing and monotone decreasing, but is not strictly
monotone (unless X consists of at most one point - why?).
Continuous functions are not necessarily monotone (consider for in-
stance the function f(x) = x2 on R), and monotone functions are not
necessarily continuous; for instance, consider the function f : [âˆ’1, 1] â†’
R deï¬ned earlier by
f(x) :=
! âˆ’1
if x â‰¤0
1
if x > 0.
Monotone functions obey the maximum principle (Exercise 9.8.1), but
not the intermediate value principle (Exercise 9.8.2). On the other hand,
it is possible for a monotone function to have many, many discontinuities
(Exercise 9.8.5).

If a function is both strictly monotone and continuous, then it has

9.9
Uniform continuity
We know that a continuous function on a closed interval [a, b] remains
bounded (and in fact attains its maximum and minimum, by the max-
imum principle). However, if we replace the closed interval by an open
interval, then continuous functions need not be bounded any more. An
example is the function f : (0, 2) â†’R deï¬ned by f(x) := 1/x. This
function is continuous at every point in (0, 2), and is hence continuous
at (0, 2), but is not bounded. Informally speaking, the problem here is
that while the function is indeed continuous at every point in the open
interval (0, 2), it becomes â€œless and lessâ€ continuous as one approaches
the endpoint 0.
Let us analyze this phenomenon further, using the â€œepsilon-deltaâ€
deï¬nition of continuity - Proposition 9.4.7(c). We know that if f : X â†’
R is continuous at a point x0, then for every Îµ > 0 there exists a Î´ such
that f(x) will be Îµ-close to f(x0) whenever x âˆˆX is Î´-close to x0. In
other words, we can force f(x) to be Îµ-close to f(x0) if we ensure that x
is suï¬ƒciently close to x0. One way of thinking about this is that around

244
9.
Continuous functions on R
every point x0 there is an â€œisland of stabilityâ€ (x0 âˆ’Î´, x0 +Î´), where the
function f(x) doesnâ€™t stray by more than Îµ from f(x0).
Example 9.9.1. Take the function f(x) := 1/x mentioned above at
the point x0 = 1. In order to ensure that f(x) is 0.1-close to f(x0), it
suï¬ƒces to take x to be 1/11-close to x0, since if x is 1/11-close to x0
then 10/11 < x < 12/11, and so 11/12 < f(x) < 11/10, and so f(x)
is 0.1-close to f(x0). Thus the â€œÎ´â€ one needs to make f(x) 0.1-close to
f(x0) is about 1/11 or so, at the point x0 = 1.
Now let us look instead at the point x0 = 0.1. The function f(x) =
1/x is still continuous here, but we shall see the continuity is much
worse. In order to ensure that f(x) is 0.1-close to f(x0), we need x to
be 1/1010-close to x0. Indeed, if x is 1/1010 close to x0, then 10/101 <
x < 102/1010, and so 9.901 < f(x) < 10.1, so f(x) is 0.1-close to f(x0).
Thus one needs a much smaller â€œÎ´â€ for the same value of Îµ - i.e., f(x) is
much more â€œunstableâ€ near 0.1 than it is near 1, in the sense that there
is a much smaller â€œisland of stabilityâ€ around 0.1 as there is around 1
(if one is interested in keeping f(x) 0.1-stable).
On the other hand, there are other continuous functions which do
not exhibit this behavior. Consider the function g : (0, 2) â†’R deï¬ned
by g(x) := 2x. Let us ï¬x Îµ = 0.1 as before, and investigate the island
of stability around x0 = 1. It is clear that if x is 0.05-close to x0, then
g(x) is 0.1-close to g(x0); in this case we can take Î´ to be 0.05 at x0 = 1.
And if we move x0 around, say if we set x0 to 0.1 instead, the Î´ does not
change - even when x0 is set to 0.1 instead of 1, we see that g(x) will
stay 0.1-close to g(x0) whenever x is 0.05-close to x0. Indeed, the same
Î´ works for every x0. When this happens, we say that the function g is
uniformly continuous. More precisely:
Deï¬nition 9.9.2 (Uniform continuity). Let X be a subset of R, and
let f : X â†’R be a function. We say that f is uniformly continuous if,
for every Îµ > 0, there exists a Î´ > 0 such that f(x) and f(x0) are Îµ-close
whenever x, x0 âˆˆX are two points in X which are Î´-close.
Remark 9.9.3. This deï¬nition should be compared with the notion
of continuity. From Proposition 9.4.7(c), we know that a function f is
continuous if for every Îµ > 0, and every x0 âˆˆX, there is a Î´ > 0 such
that f(x) and f(x0) are Îµ-close whenever x âˆˆX is Î´-close to x0. The
diï¬€erence between uniform continuity and continuity is that in uniform

Example 9.9.4. (Informal) The function f : (0, 2) â†’R deï¬ned by

Chapter 10

10.1
Basic deï¬nitions
We can now begin the rigorous treatment of calculus in earnest, starting
with the notion of a derivative. We can now deï¬ne derivatives analyti-
cally, using limits, in contrast to the geometric deï¬nition of derivatives,
which uses tangents. The advantage of working analytically is that (a)
we do not need to know the axioms of geometry, and (b) these deï¬nitions
can be modiï¬ed to handle functions of several variables, or functions
whose values are vectors instead of scalar. Furthermore, oneâ€™s geometric
intuition becomes diï¬ƒcult to rely on once one has more than three di-
mensions in play. (Conversely, one can use oneâ€™s experience in analytic
rigour to extend oneâ€™s geometric intuition to such abstract settings; as
mentioned earlier, the two viewpoints complement rather than oppose
each other.)
Deï¬nition 10.1.1 (Diï¬€erentiability at a point). Let X be a subset of
R, and let x0 âˆˆX be an element of X which is also a limit point of X.
Let f : X â†’R be a function. If the limit

x0 on X with derivative L, and write fâ€²(x0) := L. If the limit does not
exist, or if x0 is not an element of X or not a limit point of X, we leave
fâ€²(x0) undeï¬ned, and say that f is not diï¬€erentiable at x0 on X.

If a function is diï¬€erentiable at x0, then it is approximately linear

(b) For every Îµ > 0, there exists a Î´ > 0 such that f(x) is Îµ|x âˆ’x0|-

Remark 10.1.8. Newtonâ€™s approximation is of course named after the

Proof. See Exercise 10.1.2.
Remark 10.1.9. We can phrase Proposition 10.1.7 in a more informal

Proposition 10.1.10 (Diï¬€erentiability implies continuity). Let X be a

Proof. See Exercise 10.1.3.
Deï¬nition 10.1.11 (Diï¬€erentiability on a domain). Let X be a subset
of R, and let f : X â†’R be a function. We say that f is diï¬€erentiable
on X if, for every limit point x0 âˆˆX, the function f is diï¬€erentiable at
x0 on X.
From Proposition 10.1.10 and the above deï¬nition we have an im-
mediate corollary:
Corollary 10.1.12. Let X be a subset of R, and let f : X â†’R be a
function which is diï¬€erentiable on X. Then f is also continuous on X.

Now we state the basic properties of derivatives which you are all

Theorem 10.1.13 (Diï¬€erential calculus). Let X be a subset of R, let
x0 âˆˆX be a limit point of X, and let f : X â†’R and g : X â†’R be

fâ€²(x0) = 0.
(b) If f is the identity function, i.e., f(x) = x for all x âˆˆX, then f
is diï¬€erentiable at x0 and fâ€²(x0) = 1.
(c) (Sum rule) If f and g are diï¬€erentiable at x0, then f + g is also
diï¬€erentiable at x0, and (f + g)â€²(x0) = fâ€²(x0) + gâ€²(x0).
(d) (Product rule) If f and g are diï¬€erentiable at x0, then fg is also
diï¬€erentiable at x0, and (fg)â€²(x0) = fâ€²(x0)g(x0) + f(x0)gâ€²(x0).
(e) If f is diï¬€erentiable at x0 and c is a real number, then cf is also
diï¬€erentiable at x0, and (cf)â€²(x0) = cfâ€²(x0).
(f ) (Diï¬€erence rule) If f and g are diï¬€erentiable at x0, then f âˆ’g is
also diï¬€erentiable at x0, and (f âˆ’g)â€²(x0) = fâ€²(x0) âˆ’gâ€²(x0).
(g) If g is diï¬€erentiable at x0, and g is non-zero on X (i.e., g(x) Ì¸= 0
for all x âˆˆX), then 1/g is also diï¬€erentiable at x0, and ( 1

10.2
Local maxima, local minima, and derivatives
As you learnt in your basic calculus courses, one very common appli-
cation of using derivatives is to locate maxima and minima. We now
present this material again, but this time in a rigorous manner.

258
10.
Diï¬€erentiation of functions
The notion of a function f : X â†’R attaining a maximum or mini-
mum at a point x0 âˆˆX was deï¬ned in Deï¬nition 9.6.5. We now localize
this deï¬nition:
Deï¬nition 10.2.1 (Local maxima and minima). Let f : X â†’R be
a function, and let x0 âˆˆX. We say that f attains a local maximum
at x0 iï¬€there exists a Î´ > 0 such that the restriction f|Xâˆ©(x0âˆ’Î´,x0+Î´)
of f to X âˆ©(x0 âˆ’Î´, x0 + Î´) attains a maximum at x0. We say that
f attains a local minimum at x0 iï¬€there exists a Î´ > 0 such that the
restriction f|Xâˆ©(x0âˆ’Î´,x0+Î´) of f to X âˆ©(x0 âˆ’Î´, x0 +Î´) attains a minimum
at x0.
Remark 10.2.2. If f attains a maximum at x0, we sometimes say that
f attains a global maximum at x0, in order to distinguish it from the
local maxima deï¬ned here. Note that if f attains a global maximum
at x0, then it certainly also attains a local maximum at this x0, and
similarly for minima.
Example 10.2.3. Let f : R â†’R denote the function f(x) := x2 âˆ’x4.
This function does not attain a global minimum at 0, since for example
f(2) = âˆ’12 < 0 = f(0), however it does attain a local minimum, for
if we choose Î´ := 1 and restrict f to the interval (âˆ’1, 1), then for all
x âˆˆ(âˆ’1, 1) we have x4 â‰¤x2 and thus f(x) = x2 âˆ’x4 â‰¥0 = f(0), and
so f|(âˆ’1,1) has a local minimum at 0.
Example 10.2.4. Let f : Z â†’R be the function f(x) = x, deï¬ned on
the integers only. Then f has no global maximum or global minimum
(why?), but attains both a local maximum and local minimum at every
integer n (why?).
Remark 10.2.5. If f : X â†’R attains a local maximum at a point x0
in X, and Y âŠ‚X is a subset of X which contains x0, then the restriction
f|Y : Y â†’R also attains a local maximum at x0 (why?). Similarly for
minima.
The connection between local maxima, minima and derivatives is the
following.
Proposition 10.2.6 (Local extrema are stationary). Let a < b be real
numbers, and let f : (a, b) â†’R be a function. If x0 âˆˆ(a, b), f is diï¬€er-

Local maxima, local minima, and derivatives
259
entiable at x0, and f attains either a local maximum or local minimum
at x0, then fâ€²(x0) = 0.
Proof. See Exercise 10.2.1.
Note that f must be diï¬€erentiable for this proposition to work; see
Exercise 10.2.2. Also, this proposition does not work if the open interval
(a, b) is replaced by a closed interval [a, b]. For instance, the function
f : [1, 2] â†’R deï¬ned by f(x) := x has a local maximum at x0 = 2 and a
local minimum x0 = 1 (in fact, these local extrema are global extrema),
but at both points the derivative is fâ€²(x0) = 1, not fâ€²(x0) = 0. Thus
the endpoints of an interval can be local maxima or minima even if the
derivative is not zero there. Finally, the converse of this proposition is
false (Exercise 10.2.3).
By combining Proposition 10.2.6 with the maximum principle, one
can obtain
Theorem 10.2.7 (Rolleâ€™s theorem). Let a < b be real numbers, and let
g : [a, b] â†’R be a continuous function which is diï¬€erentiable on (a, b).
Suppose also that g(a) = g(b). Then there exists an x âˆˆ(a, b) such that
gâ€²(x) = 0.
Proof. See Exercise 10.2.4.
Remark 10.2.8. Note that we only assume f is diï¬€erentiable on the
open interval (a, b), though of course the theorem also holds if we assume
f is diï¬€erentiable on the closed interval [a, b], since this is larger than
(a, b).
Rolleâ€™s theorem has an important corollary.
Corollary 10.2.9 (Mean value theorem). Let a < b be real numbers,
and let f : [a, b] â†’R be a function which is continuous on [a, b] and
diï¬€erentiable on (a, b). Then there exists an x âˆˆ(a, b) such that fâ€²(x) =
f(b)âˆ’f(a)
bâˆ’a
.
Proof. See Exercise 10.2.5.

10.3
Monotone functions and derivatives
In your elementary calculus courses, you may have come across the as-
sertion that a positive derivative meant an increasing function, and a
negative derivative meant a decreasing function. This statement is not
completely accurate, but it is pretty close; we now give the precise ver-
sion of these statements below.
Proposition 10.3.1. Let X be a subset of R, let x0 âˆˆX be a limit point
of X, and let f : X â†’R be a function. If f is monotone increasing and
f is diï¬€erentiable at x0, then fâ€²(x0) â‰¥0. If f is monotone decreasing
and f is diï¬€erentiable at x0, then fâ€²(x0) â‰¤0.
Proof. See Exercise 10.3.1.
Remark 10.3.2. We have to assume that f is diï¬€erentiable at x0;
There exist monotone functions which are not always diï¬€erentiable (see
Exercise 10.3.2), and of course if f is not diï¬€erentiable at x0 we cannot
possibly conclude that fâ€²(x0) â‰¥0 or fâ€²(x0) â‰¤0.

not always the case (Exercise 10.3.3).
On the other hand, we do have a converse result: if function has
strictly positive derivative, then it must be strictly monotone increasing:
Proposition 10.3.3. Let a < b, and let f : [a, b] â†’R be a diï¬€erentiable
function. If fâ€²(x) > 0 for all x âˆˆ[a, b], then f is strictly monotone
increasing. If fâ€²(x) < 0 for all x âˆˆ[a, b], then f is strictly monotone
decreasing. If fâ€²(x) = 0 for all x âˆˆ[a, b], then f is a constant function.
Proof. See Exercise 10.3.4.

Chapter 11
The Riemann integral
In the previous chapter we reviewed diï¬€erentiation - one of the two pil-
lars of single variable calculus. The other pillar is, of course, integration,
which is the focus of the current chapter. More precisely, we will turn
to the deï¬nite integral, the integral of a function on a ï¬xed interval, as
opposed to the indeï¬nite integral, otherwise known as the antiderivative.
These two are of course linked by the Fundamental theorem of calculus,
of which more will be said later.
For us, the study of the deï¬nite integral will start with an interval I
which could be open, closed, or half-open, and a function f : I â†’R, and
will lead us to a number
	
I f; we can write this integral as
	
I f(x) dx
(of course, we could replace x by any other dummy variable), or if I has
endpoints a and b, we shall also write this integral as
	 b
a f or
	 b
a f(x) dx.
To actually deï¬ne this integral
	
I f is somewhat delicate (especially
if one does not want to assume any axioms concerning geometric notions
such as area), and not all functions f are integrable. It turns out that
there are at least two ways to deï¬ne this integral: the Riemann inte-
gral, named after Georg Riemann (1826â€“1866), which we will do here and
which suï¬ƒces for most applications, and the Lebesgue integral, named af-
ter Henri Lebesgue (1875â€“1941), which supercedes the Riemann integral
and works for a much larger class of functions. The Lebesgue integral
will be constructed in Chapter 11.45. There is also the Riemann-Steiltjes
integral
	
I f(x) dÎ±(x), a generalization of the Riemann integral due to
Thomas Stieltjes (1856â€“1894), which we will discuss in Section 11.8.
Our strategy in deï¬ning the Riemann integral is as follows. We begin
by ï¬rst deï¬ning a notion of integration on a very simple class of functions
- the piecewise constant functions. These functions are quite primitive,
but their advantage is that integration is very easy for these functions,
